Tips for building the best skincare routine for dry skin.\n\n1. What causes dry skin?\nIn dermatology, dry skin is not really a well-defined condition. Instead, it represents a heterogeneous mix of relatively unrelated changes in a skin layer called the stratum corneum. When dermatologists talk about dry skin, what they really mean is a reduction in water content in the stratum corneum. This water content level is centrally important in maintaining normal appearance and texture for your skin. However, because skin is a living part of you, moisturizing dry skin is anything but simple!\n\n2. What factors determine skin moisture levels?\nLargely, your body's moisture level depends on how you hydrate yourself. Be sure you are drinking plenty of water daily if you don't want dry, flaky skin to find you. Additionally, frequently and prolonged exposure of your skin to cleansing solvents or detergents will reduce the stratum corneum's ability to bind water. This also happens similarly with diseases like eczema and when you overuse harsh actives like retinoids. Another thing, your skin moisture is most easily altered by the skincare products you use daily. The greater the difference in hydration or moisture level of your skin versus the external environment, the faster your skin will lose water. In fact, this means your skin dries faster the colder the temperature, the more wind, and the drier the air [5]. That's why we need to apply moisturizer to keep skin as hydrated as possible.\n\n3. How should dry skin be cared for?\nTo prevent dry skin, a light moisturizer layer reduces wind impact and increases humidity around your skin. This slows the evaporation rate of water, helping it persist longer [6]. This is the most basic principle any moisturizing barrier cream works this way. Today, the science of dermatology has risen to new heights. Moisturizers now contain many more functionally active ingredients beyond just replenishing barrier moisture.\n\nAll treatments aimed at reducing dry skin focus on restoring moisture to the stratum corneum, also known as the barrier epidermis. This can only be achieved if the function of this outermost layer is first restored [7]. In medicine, removal of irritants and harsh soap products is considered the first step any dry skin patient should take. If you have eczema, you\u2019ll be more aware of the damage and drying effects of regular soaps. A UK survey found 38% of eczema sufferers had significant flares after washing with soap [7]. This is why adhering to good habits will benefit your skin far more than haphazardly using any available product.\n\nThe first step to treating dry skin in fact is using a good moisturizer. Basically, these creams provide not just a protective layer on skin to improve barrier function but also reduce uncomfortable dryness and itch [8]. Quality moisturizers contain many restorative, hydrating and soothing ingredients. They slow down evaporation of water from the skin surface and the rate water moves from deeper layers to the stratum corneum. Along with other ingredients that have emollient, smoothing effects and maintain a refreshed rather than dull appearance.\n\nIf you check the ingredients on your products, you may see some contain 'humectants'. The way humectants like hyaluronic acid actually work is by balancing moisture on the skin, indirectly hydrating it. Some other good quality moisturizers also contain vitamin A derivatives, such as retinol palmitate. These tend to become medicinal as they stimulate natural glycosaminoglycan synthesis, increasing water-holding in both the epidermis and dermis [1]. These moisturizers effectively hydrate inside the skin, slowing the increased skin cell turnover rate from chronically irritated dry skin.\n\n4. What should be noted when treating dry skin?\nThrough much research, doctors have found that most people, even those instructed by medical staff, improperly use moisturizer!. Typically, skin moisturizers are most effective when used before skin gets dry and should be reapplied regularly throughout the day for maximal benefit. When used this way, moisturizers can actually have an anti-inflammatory effect. When applying moisturizer, its thickness should guide where you use it. Thin, watery lotions should be reserved for the scalp and other frequently hairy areas. Your face, arms and chest should actually use the same moisturizer. If you have especially dry or scaly skin, this is when an ointment is more appropriate.\n\nRemember good skincare is a marathon, not a sprint - it just takes persistence and steadiness, not speed and hurriedness. Even once you feel your skin is under control, you should continue using moisturizer daily and avoid harsh soaps. If your skin is so dry it's scaly, you may have had too much irritant exposure - to relieve this intense itchiness, you can bathe in diluted 3% salt water. This can be done at home relatively cheaply, simply but provides significant rapid itch relief. Moisturizing agents like oatmeal or colloidal oatmeal extract can also be added to baths if your skin is not too sensitive.", "replyCount": 1, "retweetCount": 0, "likeCount": 1, "quoteCount": 0, "conversationId": 1712751670340485225, "hashtags": [], "cashtags": [], "mentionedUsers": [], "links": [], "viewCount": 32, "retweetedTweet": null, "quotedTweet": null, "place": null, "coordinates": null, "inReplyToTweetId": null, "inReplyToUser": null, "source": "<a href=\"https://mobile.twitter.com\" rel=\"nofollow\">Twitter Web App</a>", "sourceUrl": "https://mobile.twitter.com", "sourceLabel": "Twitter Web App", "media": {"photos": [{"url": "https://pbs.twimg.com/media/F8TocFEawAAX-zh.png"}, {"url": "https://pbs.twimg.com/media/F8TpQQFacAA4O82.png"}, {"url": "https://pbs.twimg.com/media/F8TpoW2a0AAI562.jpg"}, {"url": "https://pbs.twimg.com/media/F8TqdrjawAAoXVV.jpg"}], "videos": [], "animated": []}, "_type": "snscrape.modules.twitter.Tweet"}
